<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Cloud</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"><div class="flaggedrevs_short flaggedrevs_stable_synced plainlinks noprint nomobile" id="mw-fr-revisiontag"><div class="flaggedrevs_short_basic"><img alt="Changes must be reviewed before being displayed on this page." class="flaggedrevs-icon" src="/w/extensions/FlaggedRevs/frontend/modules/img/doc-magnify.png" title="Changes must be reviewed before being displayed on this page."/><img alt="show/hide details" class="fr-toggle-arrow" id="mw-fr-revisiontoggle" src="/w/extensions/FlaggedRevs/frontend/modules/img/arrow-down.png" style="display:none;"/></div>
<div id="mw-fr-revisiondetails-wrapper" style="position:relative;"><div class="flaggedrevs_short_details" id="mw-fr-revisiondetails" style="display:none">This is the <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Pending_changes" title="Wikipedia:Pending changes">latest accepted revision</a>, <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Special:Log&amp;type=review&amp;page=Cloud">reviewed</a> on <i>4 October 2017</i>.</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
</div>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Cloud_(disambiguation)" title="Cloud (disambiguation)">Cloud (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:442px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cumulus_clouds_panorama.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1067" data-file-width="3568" height="132" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Cumulus_clouds_panorama.jpg/440px-Cumulus_clouds_panorama.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Cumulus_clouds_panorama.jpg/660px-Cumulus_clouds_panorama.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Cumulus_clouds_panorama.jpg/880px-Cumulus_clouds_panorama.jpg 2x" width="440"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cumulus_clouds_panorama.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Cumuliform cloudscape over <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Swifts_Creek,_Victoria" title="Swifts Creek, Victoria">Swifts Creek</a>, Australia</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Meteorology" title="Meteorology">meteorology</a>, a <b>cloud</b> is an <a href="/wiki/Aerosol" title="Aerosol">aerosol</a> comprising a visible mass of minute <a href="/wiki/Liquid" title="Liquid">liquid</a> <a href="/wiki/Drop_(liquid)" title="Drop (liquid)">droplets</a>, frozen <a href="/wiki/Crystal" title="Crystal">crystals</a>, or particles suspended in the <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">atmosphere</a> above the surface of a <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">planetary</a> body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> The droplets and crystals may be made of water or various chemicals. On Earth, clouds are formed as a result of saturation of the air when it is cooled to its <a href="/wiki/Dew_point" title="Dew point">dew point</a>, or when it gains sufficient moisture (usually in the form of <a href="/wiki/Water_vapor" title="Water vapor">water vapor</a>) from an adjacent source to raise the dew point to the ambient temperature. They are seen in the Earth's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homosphere" title="Homosphere">homosphere</a> (which includes the <a href="/wiki/Troposphere" title="Troposphere">troposphere</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stratosphere" title="Stratosphere">stratosphere</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mesosphere" title="Mesosphere">mesosphere</a>). <b>Nephology</b> is the science of clouds which is undertaken in the <a href="/wiki/Cloud_physics" title="Cloud physics">cloud physics</a> branch of <a href="/wiki/Meteorology" title="Meteorology">meteorology</a>.</p>
<p>There are two methods of naming clouds in their respective layers of the atmosphere; Latin and common. Cloud types in the <a href="/wiki/Troposphere" title="Troposphere">troposphere</a>, the atmospheric layer closest to Earth's surface, have <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> names due to the universal adaptation of <a href="/wiki/Luke_Howard" title="Luke Howard">Luke Howard</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Nomenclature" title="Nomenclature">nomenclature</a>. Formally proposed in 1802, it became the basis of a modern international system that divides clouds into five physical <i>forms</i> that appear in any or all of three altitude <i>levels</i> (formerly known as <a href="/wiki/Etage" title="Etage"><i>étages</i></a>). These physical types, in approximate ascending order of convective activity, include <i>stratiform</i> sheets, <i>cirriform</i> wisps and patches, <i>stratocumuliform</i> layers (mainly structured as rolls, ripples, and patches), <i>cumuliform</i> heaps, and very large <i>cumulonimbiform</i> heaps that often show complex structure. The physical forms are divided by altitude level into ten basic genus-types. The Latin names for applicable high-level genera carry a <i>cirro</i>- prefix, and an <i>alto</i>- prefix is added to the names of the mid-level genus-types. Most of the genera can be subdivided into <i>species</i> and further subdivided into <i>varieties</i>.</p>
<p>Two cirriform clouds that form higher up in the stratosphere and mesosphere have common names for their main types. They are seen infrequently, mostly in the polar regions of Earth. Clouds have been observed in the atmospheres of other <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">planets</a> and <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">moons</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a> and beyond. However, due to their different temperature characteristics, they are often composed of other substances such as <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sulfuric_acid" title="Sulfuric acid">sulfuric acid</a> as well as water.</p>
<p>Taken as a whole, homospheric clouds can be cross-classified by form and level to derive the ten tropospheric genera and the two additional major types above the troposphere. The cumulus genus includes three species that indicate vertical size. Clouds with sufficient vertical extent to occupy more than one altitude level are officially classified as <i>low-</i> or <i>mid-level</i> according to the altitude range at which each initially forms. However they are also more informally classified as <i>multi-level</i> or <i>vertical</i>.</p>

</li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-38"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology_and_history_of_cloud_science_and_nomenclature">Etymology and history of cloud science and nomenclature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cloud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology and history of cloud science and nomenclature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_meteorology" title="Timeline of meteorology">Timeline of meteorology</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cloud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The origin of the term <i>cloud</i> can be found in the <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">old English</a> <i>clud</i> or <i>clod</i>, meaning a hill or a mass of rock. Around the beginning of the 13th century, it was extended as a <a href="/wiki/Metaphor" title="Metaphor">metaphor</a> to include rain clouds as masses of evaporated water in the sky because of the similarity in appearance between a mass of rock and a cumulus heap cloud. Over time, the metaphoric term replaced the original old English <i>weolcan</i> to refer to clouds in general.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harper_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Harper-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Aristotle_and_Theophrastus">Aristotle and Theophrastus</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cloud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Aristotle and Theophrastus">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Ancient cloud studies were not made in isolation, but were observed in combination with other <a href="/wiki/Weather" title="Weather">weather</a> elements and even other natural sciences. In about 340 BC the Greek philosopher <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> wrote <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meteorologica" title="Meteorologica">Meteorologica</a></i>, a work which represented the sum of knowledge of the time about natural science, including weather and climate. For the first time, precipitation and the clouds from which precipitation fell were called meteors, which originate from the Greek word <i>meteoros</i>, meaning 'high in the sky'. From that word came the modern term <a href="/wiki/Meteorology" title="Meteorology">meteorology</a>, the study of clouds and weather. Meteorologica was based on intuition and simple observation, but not on what is now considered the scientific method. Nevertheless, it was the first known work that attempted to treat a broad range of meteorological topics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ancient_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ancient-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="First_comprehensive_classification">First comprehensive classification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cloud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: First comprehensive classification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>After centuries of speculative theories about the formation and behavior of clouds, the first truly scientific studies were undertaken by <a href="/wiki/Luke_Howard" title="Luke Howard">Luke Howard</a> in England and <a href="/wiki/Jean-Baptiste_Lamarck" title="Jean-Baptiste Lamarck">Jean-Baptiste Lamarck</a> in France. Howard was a methodical observer with a strong grounding in the Latin language and used his background to classify the various tropospheric cloud types during 1802. He believed that the changing cloud forms in the sky could unlock the key to weather forecasting. Lamarck had worked independently on cloud classification the same year and had come up with a different naming scheme that failed to make an impression even in his home country of <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a> because it used unusual French names for cloud types. His system of nomenclature included twelve categories of clouds, with such names as (translated from French) hazy clouds, dappled clouds and broom-like clouds. By contrast, Howard used universally accepted Latin, which caught on quickly after it was published in 1803.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Preface_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Preface-5">[5]</a></sup> As a sign of the popularity of the naming scheme, the German dramatist and poet <a href="/wiki/Johann_Wolfgang_von_Goethe" title="Johann Wolfgang von Goethe">Johann Wolfgang von Goethe</a> composed four poems about clouds, dedicating them to Howard. An elaboration of Howard's system was eventually formally adopted by the International Meteorological Conference in 1891.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Preface_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-Preface-5">[5]</a></sup> This system covered only the tropospheric cloud types, but the discovery of clouds above the troposphere during the late 19th. century eventually led to the creation separate classification schemes for these very high clouds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Polar-stratospheric_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Polar-stratospheric-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Tropospheric">Tropospheric</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cloud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Tropospheric">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For a comprehensive listing of over 90 combinations of genera divided into species and subdivided into varieties with Latin etymologies, see <a href="/wiki/List_of_cloud_types" title="List of cloud types">List of cloud types</a>.</div>
<p>Tropospheric classification is based on a hierarchy of categories with physical forms and altitude levels at the top.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LANDSAT_identification_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-LANDSAT_identification-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Definitions_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Definitions-8">[8]</a></sup> These are cross-classified into a total of ten genus types, most of which can be divided into species and further subdivided into varieties which are at the bottom of the hierarchy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Principles_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Principles-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_forms">Physical forms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cloud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Physical forms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Clouds in the troposphere assume five physical forms based on structure and process of formation. These forms are commonly used for the purpose of satellite analysis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LANDSAT_identification_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-LANDSAT_identification-7">[7]</a></sup> They are given below in approximate ascending order of instability or <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_convection" title="Atmospheric convection">convective</a> activity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-meteorology_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-meteorology-10">[10]</a></sup> Two of the forms are each divided into several genera that are differentiated mainly by altitude range or level. The other three comprise just one genus type for each form.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Stratiform">Stratiform</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cloud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Stratiform">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><i>Non-convective</i> stratiform clouds appear in <i>stable</i> airmass conditions and, in general, have flat sheet-like structures that can form at any altitude in the troposphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stratiform_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stratiform-11">[11]</a></sup> Very low stratiform cloud results when <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Advection_fog" title="Advection fog">advection fog</a> is lifted above surface level during breezy conditions. The stratiform group is divided by altitude range into the genera <a href="/wiki/Cirrostratus_cloud" title="Cirrostratus cloud">cirrostratus</a> (high-level), <a href="/wiki/Altostratus_cloud" title="Altostratus cloud">altostratus</a> (mid-level), <a href="/wiki/Stratus_cloud" title="Stratus cloud">stratus</a> (low-level), and <a href="/wiki/Nimbostratus_cloud" title="Nimbostratus cloud">nimbostratus</a> (multi-level).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Definitions_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-Definitions-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Cirriform">Cirriform</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cloud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Cirriform">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Cirriform clouds are generally of the genus <a href="/wiki/Cirrus_cloud" title="Cirrus cloud">cirrus</a> and have the appearance of detached or semi-merged filaments. They form at high tropospheric altitudes in air that is mostly stable with little or no convective activity, although denser patches may occasionally show buildups caused by <i>limited</i> high-level <a href="/wiki/Convection" title="Convection">convection</a> where the air is partly <i>unstable</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cirrus_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-cirrus-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Stratocumuliform">Stratocumuliform</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cloud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Stratocumuliform">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Clouds of this structure have both cumuliform and stratiform characteristics in the form of rolls, ripples, or patches. They generally form as a result of <i>limited convection</i> in an otherwise mostly stable airmass topped by an inversion layer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Multi-regime_convection_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Multi-regime_convection-13">[13]</a></sup> If the inversion layer is absent or higher in the troposphere, increased convective activity may cause the cloud layers to develop tops in the form of turrets consisting of embedded cumuliform buildups. The stratocumuliform group is divided into <a href="/wiki/Cirrocumulus_cloud" title="Cirrocumulus cloud">cirrocumulus</a> (high-level), <a href="/wiki/Altocumulus_cloud" title="Altocumulus cloud">altocumulus</a> (mid-level), and <a href="/wiki/Stratocumulus_cloud" title="Stratocumulus cloud">stratocumulus</a> (low-level).</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Cumuliform">Cumuliform</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cloud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Cumuliform">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Cumuliform clouds generally appear in isolated heaps or tufts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USA_Today_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-USA_Today-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stommel91_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stommel91-15">[15]</a></sup> They are the product of localized but generally <i>free-convective</i> lift where there are no inversion layers in the atmosphere to limit vertical growth. In general, small cumuliform clouds tend to indicate comparatively weak instability. Larger cumuliform types are a sign of moderate to strong atmospheric instability and convective activity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mossop632-634_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mossop632-634-16">[16]</a></sup> Depending on their vertical size, clouds of the <a href="/wiki/Cumulus_cloud" title="Cumulus cloud">cumulus</a> genus type may be low-level or multi-level with moderate to towering vertical extent.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Cumulonimbiform">Cumulonimbiform</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cloud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Cumulonimbiform">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_convection" title="Atmospheric convection">Atmospheric convection</a></div>
<p>The largest free-convective clouds comprise the genus <a href="/wiki/Cumulonimbus_cloud" title="Cumulonimbus cloud">cumulonimbus</a> which are multi-level because of their towering vertical extent. They occur in highly unstable air<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-humidity.2C_saturation.2C_and_stability_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-humidity.2C_saturation.2C_and_stability-17">[17]</a></sup> and often have complex structures that include cirriform tops and multiple accessory clouds.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Levels_and_genera">Levels and genera</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cloud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Levels and genera">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Weather_map" title="Weather map">Weather map</a> and <a href="/wiki/Station_model" title="Station model">Station model</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cloud_types.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="776" data-file-width="1380" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Cloud_types.jpg/220px-Cloud_types.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Cloud_types.jpg/330px-Cloud_types.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Cloud_types.jpg/440px-Cloud_types.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cloud_types.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Genus classification by altitude of occurrence. Multi-level types not limited by altitude include the two main precipitating clouds, cumulonimbus and nimbostratus. The latter has been horizontally compressed in this depiction.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Tropospheric clouds form in any of three levels (formerly called <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%C3%A9tage" title="wikt:étage">étages</a>) based on altitude range above the Earth's surface. The grouping of clouds into levels is commonly done for the purposes of <a href="/wiki/Cloud_atlas" title="Cloud atlas">cloud atlases</a>, <a href="/wiki/Surface_weather_observation" title="Surface weather observation">surface weather observations</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Definitions_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-Definitions-8">[8]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Weather_map" title="Weather map">weather maps</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HOWTOREAD_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-HOWTOREAD-18">[18]</a></sup> The base-height range for each level varies depending on the latitudinal <a href="/wiki/Geographical_zone" title="Geographical zone">geographical zone</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NWS_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-NWS-19">[19]</a></sup> Each altitude level comprises two or three genus types differentiated mainly by physical form.</p>
<p><i>The standard levels and genus-types are summarised below in approximate descending order of the altitude at which each is normally based</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wmo-cloud_classifications_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-wmo-cloud_classifications-20">[20]</a></sup> Multi-level clouds with significant vertical extent are separately listed and summarized in approximate ascending order of instability or convective activity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-meteorology_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-meteorology-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="High-level">High-level</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cloud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: High-level">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cirrus_sky_panorama.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="1837" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Cirrus_sky_panorama.jpg/220px-Cirrus_sky_panorama.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Cirrus_sky_panorama.jpg/330px-Cirrus_sky_panorama.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Cirrus_sky_panorama.jpg/440px-Cirrus_sky_panorama.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cirrus_sky_panorama.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
High <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cirrus_uncinus" title="Cirrus uncinus">cirrus uncinus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cirrus_fibratus" title="Cirrus fibratus">cirrus fibratus</a> (see also 'species and varieties' section) upper-left merging into <a href="/wiki/Cirrostratus_fibratus" title="Cirrostratus fibratus">cirrostratus fibratus</a> with some higher <a href="/wiki/Cirrocumulus_floccus" title="Cirrocumulus floccus">cirrocumulus floccus</a> upper right</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>High clouds form at altitudes of 3,000 to 7,600 m (10,000 to 25,000 ft) in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polar_region" title="Polar region">polar regions</a>, 5,000 to 12,200 m (16,500 to 40,000 ft) in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Temperateness" title="Temperateness">temperate regions</a> and 6,100 to 18,300 m (20,000 to 60,000 ft) in the <a href="/wiki/Tropics" title="Tropics">tropics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NWS_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-NWS-19">[19]</a></sup> All cirriform clouds are classified as high and thus constitute a single genus <i>cirrus</i> (Ci). Stratocumuliform and stratiform clouds in the high altitude range carry the prefix <i>cirro-</i>, yielding the respective genus names <i>cirrocumulus</i> (Cc) and <i>cirrostratus</i> (Cs). When limited-resolution satellite images of high clouds are analysed without supporting data from direct human observations, it becomes impossible to distinguish between individual forms or genus types, which are then collectively identified as <i>high-type</i> (or informally as <i>cirrus-type</i> even though not all high clouds are of the cirrus form or genus).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cloud_type_identification_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cloud_type_identification-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>Genus cirrus (Ci):</li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dd>These are mostly fibrous wisps of delicate white cirriform ice crystal cloud that show up clearly against the blue sky.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cirrus_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-cirrus-12">[12]</a></sup> Cirrus are generally non-convective except castellanus and floccus subtypes which show limited convection. They often form along a high altitude <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jetstream" title="Jetstream">jetstream</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jetstream_clouds_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jetstream_clouds-22">[22]</a></sup> and at the very leading edge of a frontal or low-pressure disturbance where they may merge into cirrostratus. These high clouds do not produce precipitation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wmo-cloud_classifications_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-wmo-cloud_classifications-20">[20]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<ul>
<li>Genus cirrocumulus (Cc):</li>
</ul>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cirrocumulus_in_Hong_Kong.jpg"><img alt="A A large field of cirrocumulus clouds in a blue sky, beginning to merge near the upper left." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1500" data-file-width="2000" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Cirrocumulus_in_Hong_Kong.jpg/220px-Cirrocumulus_in_Hong_Kong.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Cirrocumulus_in_Hong_Kong.jpg/330px-Cirrocumulus_in_Hong_Kong.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Cirrocumulus_in_Hong_Kong.jpg/440px-Cirrocumulus_in_Hong_Kong.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cirrocumulus_in_Hong_Kong.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A large field of cirrocumulus stratiformis</div>
</div>
</div>
<dl>
<dd>This is a pure white high stratocumuliform layer of limited convection. It is composed of ice crystals or supercooled water droplets appearing as small unshaded round masses or flakes in groups or lines with ripples like sand on a beach.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cirrocumulus_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-cirrocumulus-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-YDN-364_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-YDN-364-24">[24]</a></sup> Cirrocumulus occasionally forms alongside cirrus and may be accompanied or replaced by cirrostratus clouds at the very leading edge of an active weather system.</dd>
</dl>
<ul>
<li>Genus cirrostratus (Cs):</li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dd>Cirrostratus is a thin non-convective stratiform ice crystal veil that typically gives rise to halos caused by refraction of the sun's rays. The sun and moon are visible in clear outline.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cirrostratus_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-cirrostratus-25">[25]</a></sup>Cirrostratus often thickens into altostratus ahead of a warm front or low-pressure area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Altostratus_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Altostratus-26">[26]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Mid-level">Mid-level</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cloud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Mid-level">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sunrise_(Abbottabad).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1936" data-file-width="2592" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Sunrise_%28Abbottabad%29.jpg/220px-Sunrise_%28Abbottabad%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Sunrise_%28Abbottabad%29.jpg/330px-Sunrise_%28Abbottabad%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Sunrise_%28Abbottabad%29.jpg/440px-Sunrise_%28Abbottabad%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sunrise_(Abbottabad).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Sunrise scene giving a shine to an altocumulus stratiformis perlucidus cloud (see also 'species and varieties)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Non-vertical clouds in the middle level are prefixed by <i>alto-</i>, yielding the genus names <i>altocumulus</i> (Ac) and <i>altostratus</i> (As). These clouds can form as low as 2,000 m (6,500 ft) above surface at any latitude, but may be based as high as 4,000 m (13,000 ft) near the poles, 7,000 m (23,000 ft) at mid latitudes, and 7,600 m (25,000 ft) in the tropics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NWS_19-2"><a href="#cite_note-NWS-19">[19]</a></sup> As with high clouds, it is not possible to distinguish between individual forms or genera using satellite photography alone. Without the support of human observations, these clouds are usually collectively identified as <i>middle-type</i> on satellite images.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cloud_type_identification_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cloud_type_identification-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>Genus altocumulus (Ac):</li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dd>This is a mid-level stratocumuliform cloud layer of limited convection that is usually appears in the form of irregular patches or more extensive sheets arranges in groups, lines, or waves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-altocumulus_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-altocumulus-27">[27]</a></sup> Altocumulus near the top of its range may resemble cirrocumulus but is usually thicker and composed of water droplets so that the bases show at least some light-grey shading. Opaque altocumulus associated with a weak frontal or low-pressure disturbance can produce virga, very light intermittent precipitation that evaporates before reaching the ground. If the altocumulus is mixed with moisture-laden altostratus, the precipitation may reach the ground.</dd>
</dl>
<ul>
<li>Genus altostratus (As):</li>
</ul>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:As_1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/As_1.jpg/220px-As_1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/As_1.jpg/330px-As_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/As_1.jpg/440px-As_1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:As_1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Altostratus translucidus near top of photo merging into altostratus opacus near bottom</div>
</div>
</div>
<dl>
<dd>Altostratus is a mid-level opaque or translucent non-convective veil of grey/blue-grey cloud that often forms along warm fronts and around low-pressure areas. Altostratus is usually composed of water droplets but may be mixed with ice crystals at higher altitudes. Widespread opaque altostratus can produce light continuous or intermittent precipitation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-altostratus_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-altostratus-28">[28]</a></sup> Precipitation commonly becomes heavier and more widespread if it thickens into nimbostratus.</dd>
</dl>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Low-level">Low-level</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cloud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Low-level">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Low clouds are found from near surface up to 2,000 m (6,500 ft).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NWS_19-3"><a href="#cite_note-NWS-19">[19]</a></sup> Genus types in this level either have no prefix or carry one that refers to a characteristic other than altitude. Clouds that form in the low level of the troposphere are generally of larger structure than those that form in the middle and high levels, so they can usually be identified by their forms and genus types using satellite photography alone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cloud_type_identification_21-2"><a href="#cite_note-Cloud_type_identification-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tortuga_Bay_-_Island_of_Santa_Cruz,_Galapagos.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="720" data-file-width="1280" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Tortuga_Bay_-_Island_of_Santa_Cruz%2C_Galapagos.JPG/220px-Tortuga_Bay_-_Island_of_Santa_Cruz%2C_Galapagos.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Tortuga_Bay_-_Island_of_Santa_Cruz%2C_Galapagos.JPG/330px-Tortuga_Bay_-_Island_of_Santa_Cruz%2C_Galapagos.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Tortuga_Bay_-_Island_of_Santa_Cruz%2C_Galapagos.JPG/440px-Tortuga_Bay_-_Island_of_Santa_Cruz%2C_Galapagos.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tortuga_Bay_-_Island_of_Santa_Cruz,_Galapagos.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Stratocumulus stratiformis perlucidus over <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galapagos" title="Galapagos">Galapagos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tortuga_Bay" title="Tortuga Bay">Tortuga Bay</a> (see also 'species and varieties')</div>
</div>
</div>
<ul>
<li>Genus stratocumulus (Sc):</li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dd>This genus type is a stratocumuliform cloud layer of limited convection, usually in the form of irregular patches or more extensive sheets similar to altocumulus but having larger elements with deeper-gray shading.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stratocumulus_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-stratocumulus-29">[29]</a></sup> Opaque stratocumulus can produce very light intermittent precipitation. This cloud often forms under a precipitating deck of altostratus or high-based nimbostratus associated with a well-developed warm front, slow-moving cold front, or low-pressure area. This can create the illusion of continuous precipitation of more than very light intensity falling from stratocumulus.</dd>
</dl>
<ul>
<li>Genus cumulus (Cu); species humilis – <i>little vertical extent</i>:</li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dd>These are small detached fair-weather cumuliform clouds that have nearly horizontal bases and flattened tops, and do not produce rain showers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cumulus_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-cumulus-30">[30]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<ul>
<li>Genus stratus (St):</li>
</ul>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Stratus-Cloud-Uetliberg.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1024" data-file-width="768" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Stratus-Cloud-Uetliberg.jpg/170px-Stratus-Cloud-Uetliberg.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Stratus-Cloud-Uetliberg.jpg/255px-Stratus-Cloud-Uetliberg.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Stratus-Cloud-Uetliberg.jpg/340px-Stratus-Cloud-Uetliberg.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Stratus-Cloud-Uetliberg.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Stratus nebulosus translucidus</div>
</div>
</div>
<dl>
<dd>This is a flat or sometimes ragged non-convective stratiform type that sometimes resembles elevated fog.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stratus_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-stratus-31">[31]</a></sup> Only very weak precipitation can fall from this cloud (usually drizzle or snow grains), although heavier rain or snow may fall through a stratus layer from a higher precipitating cloud deck. When a low stratiform cloud contacts the ground, it is called <a href="/wiki/Fog" title="Fog">fog</a> if the prevailing surface visibility is less than 1 kilometer, although radiation and advection types of fog tend to form in clear air rather than from stratus layers. If the visibility increases to 1 kilometer or higher in any kind of fog, the visible condensation is termed <a href="/wiki/Mist" title="Mist">mist</a>.</dd>
</dl>
<h4><span id="Multi-level_(low_to_mid-level_cloud_base)"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Multi-level_.28low_to_mid-level_cloud_base.29">Multi-level (low to mid-level cloud base)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cloud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Multi-level (low to mid-level cloud base)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ns1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Ns1.jpg/220px-Ns1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Ns1.jpg/330px-Ns1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Ns1.jpg/440px-Ns1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ns1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Moderate to deep vertical nimbostratus cloud covering the sky with a scattered layer of low stratus fractus pannus (see also 'species' and 'supplementary features' sections)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>These clouds have low to middle level bases that form anywhere from near surface to about 2,400 m (8,000 ft) and tops that can extend into the high altitude range. Nimbostratus and some cumulus in this group usually achieve moderate or deep vertical extent, but without towering structure. However, with sufficient airmass instability, upward-growing cumuliform clouds can grow to high towering proportions. Although genus types with vertical extent are often informally considered a single group,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cloud_atlas_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-cloud_atlas-32">[32]</a></sup> the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/International_Civil_Aviation_Organization_(ICAO)" title="International Civil Aviation Organization (ICAO)">International Civil Aviation Organization (ICAO)</a> distinguishes towering vertical clouds more formally as a separate group or sub-group. It is specified that these very large cumuliform and cumulonimbiform types must be identified by their standard names or abbreviations in all aviation observations (METARS) and forecasts (TAFS) to warn pilots of possible severe weather and turbulence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-automated_Cb_and_Tcu_detection_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-automated_Cb_and_Tcu_detection-33">[33]</a></sup> Multi-level clouds are of even larger structure than low clouds, and are therefore identifiable by their forms and genera, (and even species in the case of cumulus congestus) using satellite photography.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cloud_type_identification_21-3"><a href="#cite_note-Cloud_type_identification-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dd><b>Moderate and deep vertical</b></dd>
</dl>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Clouds_over_Africa.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2348" data-file-width="3522" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Clouds_over_Africa.jpg/220px-Clouds_over_Africa.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Clouds_over_Africa.jpg/330px-Clouds_over_Africa.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Clouds_over_Africa.jpg/440px-Clouds_over_Africa.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Clouds_over_Africa.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cumulus_humilis" title="Cumulus humilis">Cumulus humilis</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cumulus_mediocris" title="Cumulus mediocris">cumulus mediocris</a> with stratocumulus stratiformis perlucidus in the foreground (see also 'species and varieties')</div>
</div>
</div>
<ul>
<li>Genus nimbostratus (Ns):</li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dd>This is a diffuse dark-grey non-convective stratiform layer with great horizontal extent and moderate to deep vertical development. It lacks towering structure and looks feebly illuminated from the inside.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nimbostratus_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-nimbostratus-34">[34]</a></sup> Nimbostratus normally forms from mid-level altostratus, and develops at least moderate vertical extent<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cloud_atlas_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-cloud_atlas-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Plymouth_State_Meteorology_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Plymouth_State_Meteorology-35">[35]</a></sup> when the base subsides into the low level during precipitation that can reach moderate to heavy intensity. It commonly achieves deep vertical development when it simultaneously grows upward into the high level due to large scale frontal or cyclonic lift.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-glossary_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-glossary-36">[36]</a></sup> The <i>nimbo-</i> prefix refers to its ability to produce continuous rain or snow over a wide area, especially ahead of a warm front.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-precipitating_clouds_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-precipitating_clouds-37">[37]</a></sup> This thick cloud layer may be accompanied by embedded towering cumuliform or cumulonimbiform types.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Plymouth_State_Meteorology_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-Plymouth_State_Meteorology-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nimbostratus_associated_with_deep_convection_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-nimbostratus_associated_with_deep_convection-38">[38]</a></sup> Meteorologists affiliated with the <a href="/wiki/World_Meteorological_Organization" title="World Meteorological Organization">World Meteorological Organization</a> (WMO) officially classify nimbostratus as mid-level for synoptic purposes while informally characterizing it as multi-level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Definitions_8-3"><a href="#cite_note-Definitions-8">[8]</a></sup> Independent meteorologists and educators appear split between those who largely follow the WMO model<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cloud_atlas_32-2"><a href="#cite_note-cloud_atlas-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Plymouth_State_Meteorology_35-2"><a href="#cite_note-Plymouth_State_Meteorology-35">[35]</a></sup> and those who classify nimbostratus as low-level, despite its considerable vertical extent and its usual initial formation in the middle altitude range.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-classification_of_clouds_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-classification_of_clouds-40">[40]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<ul>
<li>Genus cumulus (Cu); species mediocris – <i>moderate vertical extent</i>:</li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dd>These cumuliform clouds of free convection have clear-cut medium-grey flat bases and white domed tops in the form of small sproutings and generally do not produce precipitation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cumulus_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-cumulus-30">[30]</a></sup> They usually form in the low level of the troposphere except during conditions of very low relative humidity when the clouds bases can rise into the middle altitude range. Moderate cumulus is officially classified as low-level and more informally characterized as having vertical extent that can involve more than one altitude level.</dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dd><b>Towering vertical</b></dd>
</dl>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_convection" title="Atmospheric convection">Atmospheric convection</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Towering_Vertical_Cloud_1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2397" data-file-width="2112" height="193" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Towering_Vertical_Cloud_1.jpg/170px-Towering_Vertical_Cloud_1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Towering_Vertical_Cloud_1.jpg/255px-Towering_Vertical_Cloud_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Towering_Vertical_Cloud_1.jpg/340px-Towering_Vertical_Cloud_1.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Towering_Vertical_Cloud_1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Towering vertical cumulus congestus embedded within a layer of cumulus mediocris. Higher layer of stratocumulus stratiformis perlucidus.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>These clouds are sometimes classified separately from the other vertical or multi-level types because of their ability to produce severe turbulence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-automated_Cb_and_Tcu_detection_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-automated_Cb_and_Tcu_detection-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>Genus cumulus (Cu); species congestus – <i>great vertical extent</i>:</li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dd>Increasing airmass instability can cause free-convective cumulus to grow very tall to the extent that the vertical height from base to top is greater than the base-width of the cloud. The cloud base takes on a darker grey coloration and the top commonly resembles a cauliflower. This cloud type can produce moderate to heavy showers<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cumulus_30-2"><a href="#cite_note-cumulus-30">[30]</a></sup> and is designated <i>Towering cumulus</i> (Tcu) by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ICAO" title="ICAO">ICAO</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-automated_Cb_and_Tcu_detection_33-2"><a href="#cite_note-automated_Cb_and_Tcu_detection-33">[33]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<ul>
<li>Genus cumulonimbus (Cb):</li>
</ul>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For information about the life-cycle or stages of a cumulonimbus, see <a href="/wiki/Cumulonimbus_cloud" title="Cumulonimbus cloud">Cumulonimbus cloud</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Towering_Verticle_Thunderhead.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2471" data-file-width="4288" height="127" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Towering_Verticle_Thunderhead.jpg/220px-Towering_Verticle_Thunderhead.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Towering_Verticle_Thunderhead.jpg/330px-Towering_Verticle_Thunderhead.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Towering_Verticle_Thunderhead.jpg/440px-Towering_Verticle_Thunderhead.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Towering_Verticle_Thunderhead.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Isolated cumulonimbus cloud over the <a href="/wiki/Mojave_Desert" title="Mojave Desert">Mojave Desert</a>, releasing a heavy shower</div>
</div>
</div>
<dl>
<dd>This genus type is a heavy towering cumulonimbiform mass of free convective cloud with a dark-grey to nearly black base and a very high top in the form of a mountain or huge tower.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cumulonimbus_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-cumulonimbus-41">[41]</a></sup> Cumulonimbus can produce <a href="/wiki/Thunderstorm" title="Thunderstorm">thunderstorms</a>, local very heavy downpours of <a href="/wiki/Rain" title="Rain">rain</a> that may cause <a href="/wiki/Flash_flood" title="Flash flood">flash floods</a>, and a variety of types of <a href="/wiki/Lightning" title="Lightning">lightning</a> including cloud-to-ground that can cause <a href="/wiki/Wildfire" title="Wildfire">wildfires</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Scott2000_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Scott2000-42">[42]</a></sup> Other convective severe weather may or may not be associated with thunderstorms and include heavy <a href="/wiki/Snow" title="Snow">snow</a> showers, <a href="/wiki/Hail" title="Hail">hail</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ncar_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-ncar-43">[43]</a></sup> strong <a href="/wiki/Wind_shear" title="Wind shear">wind shear</a>, <a href="/wiki/Downburst" title="Downburst">downbursts</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Tornado" title="Tornado">tornadoes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Renno_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Renno-45">[45]</a></sup> Of all these possible cumulonimbus-related events, lightning is the only one of these that requires a thunderstorm to be taking place since it is the lightning that creates the thunder. Cumulonimbus clouds can form in unstable airmass conditions, but tend to be more concentrated and intense when they are associated with unstable <a href="/wiki/Cold_front" title="Cold front">cold fronts</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-G.26N:207-212_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-G.26N:207-212-46">[46]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Species_and_varieties">Species and varieties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cloud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Species and varieties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lenticular_Cloud_in_Wyoming_0034b.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="3008" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/5/54/Lenticular_Cloud_in_Wyoming_0034b.jpg/220px-Lenticular_Cloud_in_Wyoming_0034b.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/5/54/Lenticular_Cloud_in_Wyoming_0034b.jpg/330px-Lenticular_Cloud_in_Wyoming_0034b.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/5/54/Lenticular_Cloud_in_Wyoming_0034b.jpg/440px-Lenticular_Cloud_in_Wyoming_0034b.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lenticular_Cloud_in_Wyoming_0034b.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Lenticular_cloud" title="Lenticular cloud">Altocumulus lenticularis</a> forming over mountains in Wyoming with lower layer of cumulus mediocris and higher layer of cirrus spissatus</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Genus types are commonly divided into subtypes called <i>species</i> that indicate specific structural details which can vary according to the stability and windshear characteristics of the atmosphere at any given time and location. Despite this hierarchy, a particular species may be a subtype of more than one genus, especially if the genera are of the same physical form and are differentiated from each other mainly by altitude or level. There are a few species, each of which can be associated with genera of more than one physical form.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-species_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-species-47">[47]</a></sup> The species types are grouped below according to the physical forms and genera with which each is normally associated. The forms, genera, and species are listed in approximate ascending order of instability or convective activity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-meteorology_10-2"><a href="#cite_note-meteorology-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>Genus and species types are further subdivided into <i>varieties</i> whose names can appear after the species name to provide a fuller description of a cloud. Some cloud varieties are not restricted to a specific altitude level or form, and can therefore be common to more than one genus or species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-varieties_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-varieties-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Species">Species</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cloud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Species">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><b>Stable or mostly stable</b> Of the stratiform group, high-level cirrostratus comprises two species. Cirrostratus <i>nebulosus</i> has a rather diffuse appearance lacking in structural detail.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nebulosus_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-nebulosus-49">[49]</a></sup> Cirrostratus <i>fibratus</i> is a species made of semi-merged filaments that are transitional to or from cirrus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fibratus_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-fibratus-50">[50]</a></sup> Mid-level altostratus and multi-level nimbostratus always have a flat or diffuse appearance and are therefore not subdivided into species. Low stratus is of the species nebulosus<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nebulosus_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-nebulosus-49">[49]</a></sup> except when broken up into ragged sheets of stratus <a href="/wiki/Fractus_cloud" title="Fractus cloud">fractus</a> (see below).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cloud_atlas_32-3"><a href="#cite_note-cloud_atlas-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-species_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-species-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-clouds_-_species_and_varieties_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-clouds_-_species_and_varieties-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<p>Cirriform clouds have three non-convective species that can form in mostly <i>stable</i> airmass conditions. Cirrus fibratus comprise filaments that may be straight, wavy, or occasionally twisted by non-convective wind shear.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fibratus_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-fibratus-50">[50]</a></sup> The species <i>uncinus</i> is similar but has upturned hooks at the ends. Cirrus <i>spissatus</i> appear as opaque patches that can show light grey shading.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-species_47-2"><a href="#cite_note-species-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<p>Stratocumuliform genus-types (cirrocumulus, altocumulus, and stratocumulus) that appear in mostly stable air have two species each. The <i>stratiformis</i> species normally occur in extensive sheets or in smaller patches where there is only minimal convective activity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stratiformis_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-stratiformis-52">[52]</a></sup> Clouds of the <i>lenticularis</i> species tend to have lens-like shapes tapered at the ends. They are most commonly seen as orographic mountain-<a href="/wiki/Wave_cloud" title="Wave cloud">wave clouds</a>, but can occur anywhere in the troposphere where there is strong wind shear combined with sufficient airmass stability to maintain a generally flat cloud structure. These two species can be found in the high, middle, or low level of the troposphere depending on the stratocumuliform genus or genera present at any given time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cloud_atlas_32-4"><a href="#cite_note-cloud_atlas-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-species_47-3"><a href="#cite_note-species-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-clouds_-_species_and_varieties_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-clouds_-_species_and_varieties-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<p><b>Ragged</b> The species <i>fractus</i> shows <i>variable</i> instability because it can be a subdivision of genus-types of different physical forms that have different stability characteristics. This subtype can be in the form of ragged but mostly <i>stable</i> stratiform sheets (stratus fractus) or small ragged cumuliform heaps with somewhat greater instability (cumulus fractus).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-species_47-4"><a href="#cite_note-species-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-clouds_-_species_and_varieties_51-2"><a href="#cite_note-clouds_-_species_and_varieties-51">[51]</a></sup> When they form at low altitudes, stratiform and cumuliform genus-types can be torn up into shreds by brisk low level winds that create mechanical turbulence against the ground. Fractus clouds can form in precipitation at low altitudes, with or without brisk or gusty winds. They are closely associated with precipitating cloud systems of considerable vertical and sometimes horizontal extent, so they are also classified as <i>accessory clouds</i> under the name <i>pannus</i> (see section on supplementary features).</p>
<p><b>Partly unstable</b> These species are subdivisions of genus types that occur in partly unstable air. The species <i>castellanus</i> appears when a mostly stable stratocumuliform or cirriform layer becomes disturbed by localized areas of airmass instability, usually in the morning or afternoon. This results in the formation of cumuliform buildups arising from a common stratiform base.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Elevated_convection_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Elevated_convection-53">[53]</a></sup> Castellanus resembles the turrets of a castle when viewed from the side, and can be found with stratocumuliform genera at any tropospheric altitude level and with limited-convective patches of high-level cirrus. Tufted clouds of the more detached <i>floccus</i> species are subdivisions of genus-types which may be cirriform or stratocumuliform in overall structure. They are sometimes seen with cirrus, and with tufted cirrocumulus, altocumulus, and stratocumulus. Low and mid level castellanus or floccus can resemble <i>cumulo</i>- and <i>cumulonimbogenitus</i> formations (see 'Mother clouds' section). However, the latter genitus types are seen mostly in the evening and are the result of cumuliform and cumulonimbiform clouds dissipating rather than forming or building.</p>
<p>A newly recognized species of stratocumulus or altocumulus has been given the name <i>volutus</i>, a roll cloud that can occur ahead of a cumulonimbus formation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ICA2017_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-ICA2017-54">[54]</a></sup> There are some volutus clouds that form as a consequence of interactions with specific geographical features rather than with a parent cloud. Perhaps the strangest geographically specific cloud of this type is the <a href="/wiki/Morning_Glory_cloud" title="Morning Glory cloud">Morning Glory</a>, a rolling cylindrical cloud that appears unpredictably over the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Carpentaria" title="Gulf of Carpentaria">Gulf of Carpentaria</a> in <a href="/wiki/Northern_Australia" title="Northern Australia">Northern Australia</a>. Associated with a powerful "ripple" in the atmosphere, the cloud may be "surfed" in <a href="/wiki/Glider_(sailplane)" title="Glider (sailplane)">glider</a> aircraft.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stg_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-stg-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<p><b>Unstable or mostly unstable</b> More general airmass instability in the troposphere tends to produce clouds of the more freely convective cumulus genus type, whose species are mainly indicators of degrees of atmospheric instability and resultant vertical development of the clouds. A cumulus cloud initially forms in the low level of the troposphere as a cloudlet of the species <i>humilis</i> that shows only slight vertical development. If the air becomes more unstable, the cloud tends to grow vertically into the species <i>mediocris</i>, then <i>congestus</i>, the tallest cumulus species<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-species_47-5"><a href="#cite_note-species-47">[47]</a></sup> which is the same type that the International Civil Aviation Organization refers to as 'towering cumulus'.</p>
<p>With highly unstable atmospheric conditions, large cumulus may continue to grow into cumulonimbus <i>calvus</i> (essentially a very tall congestus cloud that produces thunder), then ultimately into the species <i>capillatus</i> when supercooled water droplets at the top of the cloud turn into ice crystals giving it a cirriform appearance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-species_47-6"><a href="#cite_note-species-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-clouds_-_species_and_varieties_51-3"><a href="#cite_note-clouds_-_species_and_varieties-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Varieties">Varieties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cloud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Varieties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><b>Opacity-based</b></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sleepy_Twilight.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2291" data-file-width="4288" height="118" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Sleepy_Twilight.jpg/220px-Sleepy_Twilight.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Sleepy_Twilight.jpg/330px-Sleepy_Twilight.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Sleepy_Twilight.jpg/440px-Sleepy_Twilight.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sleepy_Twilight.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A layer of stratocumulus stratiformis perlucidus hiding the setting sun with a background layer of stratocumulus cumulogenitus resembling distant mountains.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>All cloud varieties fall into one of two main groups. One group identifies the opacities of particular low and mid-level cloud structures and comprises the varieties <i>translucidus</i> (thin translucent), <i>perlucidus</i> (thick opaque with translucent or very small clear breaks), and <i>opacus</i> (thick opaque). These varieties are always identifiable for cloud genera and species with variable opacity. All three are associated with the stratiformis species of altocumulus and stratocumulus. However, only two varieties are seen with altostratus and stratus nebulosus whose uniform structures prevent the formation of a perlucidus variety. Opacity-based varieties are not applied to high clouds because they are always translucent, or in the case of cirrus spissatus, always opaque.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-varieties_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-varieties-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aerographer2012_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Aerographer2012-56">[56]</a></sup> Similarly, these varieties are also not associated with moderate and towering vertical clouds because they are always opaque.</p>
<p><b>Pattern-based</b></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sculpting_La_Silla%E2%80%99s_Skies.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="3888" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Sculpting_La_Silla%E2%80%99s_Skies.jpg/220px-Sculpting_La_Silla%E2%80%99s_Skies.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Sculpting_La_Silla%E2%80%99s_Skies.jpg/330px-Sculpting_La_Silla%E2%80%99s_Skies.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Sculpting_La_Silla%E2%80%99s_Skies.jpg/440px-Sculpting_La_Silla%E2%80%99s_Skies.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sculpting_La_Silla%E2%80%99s_Skies.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Cirrus fibratus radiatus over ESO's <a href="/wiki/La_Silla_Observatory" title="La Silla Observatory">La Silla Observatory</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A second group describes the occasional arrangements of cloud structures into particular patterns that are discernible by a surface-based observer (cloud fields usually being visible only from a significant altitude above the formations). These varieties are not always present with the genera and species with which they are otherwise associated, but only appear when atmospheric conditions favor their formation. <i>Intortus</i> and <i>vertebratus</i> varieties occur on occasion with cirrus fibratus. They are respectively filaments twisted into irregular shapes, and those that are arranged in fishbone patterns, usually by uneven wind currents that favor the formation of these varieties. The variety <i>radiatus</i> is associated with cloud rows of a particular type that appear to converge at the horizon. It is sometimes seen with the fibratus and uncinus species of cirrus, the stratiformis species of altocumulus and stratocumulus, the mediocris and sometimes humilis species of cumulus,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cumulus_Humilis-Skynews_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cumulus_Humilis-Skynews-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pretor-Pinney20_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pretor-Pinney20-59">[59]</a></sup> and with the genus altostratus.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Morning_Sky_7.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2816" data-file-width="2112" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Morning_Sky_7.jpg/170px-Morning_Sky_7.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Morning_Sky_7.jpg/255px-Morning_Sky_7.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Morning_Sky_7.jpg/340px-Morning_Sky_7.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Morning_Sky_7.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Altocumulus stratiformis duplicatus at sunrise in the California Mojave Desert, USA (higher layer orange to white; lower layer grey)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Another variety, <i>duplicatus</i> (closely spaced layers of the same type, one above the other), is sometimes found with cirrus of both the fibratus and uncinus species, and with altocumulus and stratocumulus of the species stratiformis and lenticularis. The variety <i>undulatus</i> (having a wavy undulating base) can occur with any clouds of the species stratiformis or lenticularis, and with altostratus. It is only rarely observed with stratus nebulosus. The variety <i>lacunosus</i> is caused by localized downdrafts that create circular holes in the form of a honeycomb or net. It is occasionally seen with cirrocumulus and altocumulus of the species stratiformis, castellanus, and floccus, and with stratocumulus of the species stratiformis and castellanus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-varieties_48-2"><a href="#cite_note-varieties-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aerographer2012_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-Aerographer2012-56">[56]</a></sup> <b>Combinations</b> It is possible for some species to show combined varieties at one time, especially if one variety is opacity-based and the other is pattern-based. An example of this would be a layer of altocumulus stratiformis arranged in seemingly converging rows separated by small breaks. The full technical name of a cloud in this configuration would be <i>altocumulus stratiformis radiatus perlucidus</i>, which would identify respectively its genus, species, and two combined varieties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-clouds_-_species_and_varieties_51-4"><a href="#cite_note-clouds_-_species_and_varieties-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-varieties_48-3"><a href="#cite_note-varieties-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aerographer2012_56-2"><a href="#cite_note-Aerographer2012-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span id="Accessory_clouds,_supplementary_features,_and_other_derivative_types"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Accessory_clouds.2C_supplementary_features.2C_and_other_derivative_types">Accessory clouds, supplementary features, and other derivative types</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cloud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Accessory clouds, supplementary features, and other derivative types">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_cloud_types" title="List of cloud types">List of cloud types</a></div>
<p>Supplementary features and accessory clouds are not further subdivisions of cloud types below the species and variety level. Rather, they are either <i>hydrometeors</i> or special cloud types with their own Latin names that form in association with certain cloud genera, species, and varieties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-clouds_-_species_and_varieties_51-5"><a href="#cite_note-clouds_-_species_and_varieties-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aerographer2012_56-3"><a href="#cite_note-Aerographer2012-56">[56]</a></sup> Supplementary features, whether in the form of clouds or precipitation, are directly attached to the main genus-cloud. Accessory clouds, by contrast, are generally detached from the main cloud.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-features_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-features-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Precipitation-based_supplementary_features">Precipitation-based supplementary features</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cloud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Precipitation-based supplementary features">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>One group of supplementary features are not actual cloud formations, but precipitation that falls when water droplets or ice crystals that make up visible clouds have grown too heavy to remain aloft. <i>Virga</i> is a feature seen with clouds producing precipitation that evaporates before reaching the ground, these being of the genera cirrocumulus, altocumulus, altostratus, nimbostratus, stratocumulus, cumulus, and cumulonimbus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-features_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-features-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<p>When the precipitation reaches the ground without completely evaporating, it is designated as the feature <i>praecipitatio</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunlop77-78_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dunlop77-78-61">[61]</a></sup> This normally occurs with altostratus opacus, which can produce widespread but usually light precipitation, and with thicker clouds that show significant vertical development. Of the latter, <i>upward-growing</i> cumulus mediocris produces only isolated light showers, while <i>downward growing</i> nimbostratus is capable of heavier, more extensive precipitation. Towering vertical clouds have the greatest ability to produce intense precipitation events, but these tend to be localized unless organized along fast-moving cold fronts. Showers of moderate to heavy intensity can fall from cumulus congestus clouds. Cumulonimbus, the largest of all cloud genera, has the capacity to produce very heavy showers. Low stratus clouds usually produce only light precipitation, but this always occurs as the feature praecipitatio due to the fact this cloud genus lies too close to the ground to allow for the formation of virga.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-clouds_-_species_and_varieties_51-6"><a href="#cite_note-clouds_-_species_and_varieties-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aerographer2012_56-4"><a href="#cite_note-Aerographer2012-56">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-features_60-2"><a href="#cite_note-features-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Cloud-based_supplementary_features">Cloud-based supplementary features</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cloud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Cloud-based supplementary features">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><i>Incus</i> is the most type-specific supplementary feature, seen only with cumulonimbus of the species capillatus. A <a href="/wiki/Cumulonimbus_incus" title="Cumulonimbus incus">cumulonimbus incus</a> cloud top is one that has spread out into a clear anvil shape as a result of rising air currents hitting the stability layer at the <a href="/wiki/Tropopause" title="Tropopause">tropopause</a> where the air no longer continues to get colder with increasing altitude.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cumulonimbus-incus_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-cumulonimbus-incus-62">[62]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <i>mamma</i> feature forms on the bases of clouds as downward-facing bubble-like protuberances caused by localized downdrafts within the cloud. It is also sometimes called <i>mammatus</i>, an earlier version of the term used before a standardization of Latin nomenclature brought about by the World Meterorological Organization during the 20th century. The best-known is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cumulonimbus_with_mammatus" title="Cumulonimbus with mammatus">cumulonimbus with mammatus</a>, but the mamma feature is also seen occasionally with cirrus, cirrocumulus, altocumulus, altostratus, and stratocumulus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-features_60-3"><a href="#cite_note-features-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<p>A <i>tuba</i> feature is a cloud column that may hang from the bottom of a cumulus or cumulonimbus. A newly formed or poorly organized column might be comparatively benign, but can quickly intensify into a funnel cloud or tornado.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-features_60-4"><a href="#cite_note-features-60">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aerographer_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Aerographer-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunlop79_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dunlop79-64">[64]</a></sup></p>
<p>An <i><a href="/wiki/Arcus_cloud" title="Arcus cloud">arcus</a></i> feature is a roll cloud with ragged edges attached to the lower front part of cumulus congestus or cumulonimbus that forms along the leading edge of a squall line or thunderstorm outflow.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ludlum473_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ludlum473-65">[65]</a></sup> A large arcus formation can have the appearance of a dark menacing arch.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-features_60-5"><a href="#cite_note-features-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<p>Several new supplementary features have been formally recognized by the <a href="/wiki/World_Meteorological_Organization" title="World Meteorological Organization">World Meteorological Organization</a> (WMO). The feature <i>fluctus</i> can form under conditions of strong atmospheric wind shear and instability when wave-like clouds of the variety undulatus break into regularly spaced crests. This variant is sometimes known informally as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kelvin%E2%80%93Helmholtz_cloud" title="Kelvin–Helmholtz cloud">Kelvin–Helmholtz (wave) cloud</a>. This phenomenon has also been observed in cloud formations over other planets and even in the sun's atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> Another highly disturbed but more chaotic wave-like cloud feature associated with the undulatus variety has been given the Latin name <i>asperitus</i>. The supplementary feature <i>cavum</i> is a circular fall-streak hole that occasionally forms in a thin layer of supercooled altocumulus or cirrocumulus. Fall streaks consisting of virga or wisps of cirrus are usually seen beneath the hole as ice crystals fall out to a lower altitude. This type of hole is usually larger than typical lacunosus holes. A <i>murus</i> feature is a cumulonimbus wall cloud with a lowering, rotating cloud base than can lead to the development of tornadoes. A <i>cauda</i> feature is a tail cloud that extends horizontally away from the murus cloud and is the result of air feeding into the storm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ICA2017_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-ICA2017-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Accessory_clouds">Accessory clouds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cloud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Accessory clouds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Supplementary cloud formations detached from the main cloud are known as <a href="/wiki/Accessory_cloud" title="Accessory cloud">accessory clouds</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-clouds_-_species_and_varieties_51-7"><a href="#cite_note-clouds_-_species_and_varieties-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aerographer2012_56-5"><a href="#cite_note-Aerographer2012-56">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-features_60-6"><a href="#cite_note-features-60">[60]</a></sup> The heavier precipitating clouds, nimbostratus, towering cumulus (cumulus congestus), and cumulonimbus typically see the formation in precipitation of the <i>pannus</i> feature, low ragged clouds of the genera and species cumulus fractus or stratus fractus.</p>
