<p>A group of accessory clouds comprise formations that are associated mainly with upward-growing cumuliform and cumulonimbiform clouds of free convection. <i>Pileus</i> is a cap cloud that can form over a cumulonimbus or large cumulus cloud,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GarretI_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-GarretI-67">[67]</a></sup> whereas a <i>velum</i> feature is a thin horizontal sheet that sometimes forms like an apron around the middle or in front of the parent cloud.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-features_60-7"><a href="#cite_note-features-60">[60]</a></sup> An accessory cloud recently officially recognized the World meteorological Organization is the <i>flumen</i>, also known more informally as the <i>beaver's tail</i>. It is formed by the warm, humid <a href="/wiki/Inflow_(meteorology)" title="Inflow (meteorology)">inflow</a> of a super-cell thunderstorm, and can be mistaken for a tornado. Although the flumen can indicate a tornado risk, it is similar in appearance to pannus or <a href="/wiki/Scud_(cloud)" title="Scud (cloud)">scud</a> clouds and does not rotate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ICA2017_54-2"><a href="#cite_note-ICA2017-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Mother_clouds">Mother clouds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cloud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Mother clouds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Port_of_Piraeus_Panoramic_View.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2448" data-file-width="3264" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Port_of_Piraeus_Panoramic_View.JPG/220px-Port_of_Piraeus_Panoramic_View.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Port_of_Piraeus_Panoramic_View.JPG/330px-Port_of_Piraeus_Panoramic_View.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Port_of_Piraeus_Panoramic_View.JPG/440px-Port_of_Piraeus_Panoramic_View.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Port_of_Piraeus_Panoramic_View.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Cumulus partly spreading into stratocumulus cumulogenitus over the port of <a href="/wiki/Piraeus" title="Piraeus">Piraeus</a> in Greece</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Clouds initially form in clear air or become clouds when fog rises above surface level. The genus of a newly formed cloud is determined mainly by air mass characteristics such as stability and moisture content. If these characteristics change over time, the genus tends to change accordingly. When this happens, the original genus is called a <i>mother cloud</i>. If the mother cloud retains much of its original form after the appearance of the new genus, it is termed a <i>genitus</i> cloud. One example of this is <i>stratocumulus cumulogenitus</i>, a stratocumulus cloud formed by the partial spreading of a cumulus type when there is a loss of convective lift. If the mother cloud undergoes a complete change in genus, it is considered to be a <i>mutatus</i> cloud.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mother-clouds_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-mother-clouds-68">[68]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sunset_and_Clouds.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2748" data-file-width="3664" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Sunset_and_Clouds.jpg/220px-Sunset_and_Clouds.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Sunset_and_Clouds.jpg/330px-Sunset_and_Clouds.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Sunset_and_Clouds.jpg/440px-Sunset_and_Clouds.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sunset_and_Clouds.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Cumulonimbus mother cloud dissipating into stratocumulus cumulonimbogenitus at dusk</div>
</div>
</div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_genitus_and_mutatus_clouds">Other genitus and mutatus clouds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cloud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Other genitus and mutatus clouds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The genitus and mutatus categories have been expanded to include certain types that do not originate from pre-existing clouds. The term <i>flammagenitus</i> (Latin for 'fire-made') applies to cumulus congestus or cumulonimbus that are formed by large scale fires or volcanic eruptions. Smaller low-level "pyrocumulus" or "fumulus" clouds formed by contained industrial activity are now classified as cumulus <i>homogenitus</i> (Latin for 'man-made'). <a href="/wiki/Contrail" title="Contrail">Contrails</a> formed from the exhaust of aircraft flying in the upper level of the troposphere can persist and spread into formations resembling any of the high cloud genus-types and are now officially designated as cirrus, cirrostratus, or cirrocumulus homogenitus. If a homogenitus cloud of one genus changes to another genus type, it is then termed a <i>homomutatus</i> cloud. Stratus cataractagenitus (Latin for 'cataract-made') are generated by the spray from waterfalls. <i>Silvagenitus</i> (Latin for 'forest-made') is a stratus cloud that forms as water vapor is added to the air above a forest canopy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ICA2017_54-3"><a href="#cite_note-ICA2017-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Stratocumulus_fields">Stratocumulus fields</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cloud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Stratocumulus fields">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Stratocumulus clouds can be organized into "fields" that take on certain specially classified shapes and characteristics. In general, these fields are more discernible from high altitudes than from ground level. They can often be found in the following forms:</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Actinoform_cloud" title="Actinoform cloud">Actinoform</a>, which resembles a leaf or a spoked wheel.</li>
<li>Closed cell, which is cloudy in the center and clear on the edges, similar to a filled <a href="/wiki/Honeycomb" title="Honeycomb">honeycomb</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-marine_clouds_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-marine_clouds-69">[69]</a></sup></li>
<li>Open cell, which resembles an empty honeycomb, with clouds around the edges and clear, open space in the middle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Vortex_streets">Vortex streets</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cloud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Vortex streets">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Karman_vortex_street" title="Karman vortex street">Karman vortex street</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Whirpool_Clouds.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2848" data-file-width="4288" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Whirpool_Clouds.jpg/220px-Whirpool_Clouds.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Whirpool_Clouds.jpg/330px-Whirpool_Clouds.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Whirpool_Clouds.jpg/440px-Whirpool_Clouds.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Whirpool_Clouds.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Cirrus fibratus intortus formed into a Kármán vortex street at evening twilight</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>These patterns are formed from a phenomenon known as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/K%C3%A1rm%C3%A1n_vortex" title="Kármán vortex">Kármán vortex</a> which is named after the engineer and fluid dynamicist <a href="/wiki/Theodore_von_K%C3%A1rm%C3%A1n" title="Theodore von Kármán">Theodore von Kármán</a>,.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> When wind driven clouds are forced through a mountain range, or when ocean wind driven clouds encounter a high elevation island, they can begin to circle the mountain or high land mass. They can form at any altitude in the troposphere and are not restricted to any particular cloud type.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Formation_and_distribution">Formation and distribution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cloud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Formation and distribution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="How_air_becomes_saturated">How air becomes saturated</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cloud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: How air becomes saturated">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cloud_physics" title="Cloud physics">Cloud physics</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:502px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Typical_Lifecycle_of_a_Single_Cell_Thunderstorm.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1182" data-file-width="3543" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Typical_Lifecycle_of_a_Single_Cell_Thunderstorm.png/500px-Typical_Lifecycle_of_a_Single_Cell_Thunderstorm.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Typical_Lifecycle_of_a_Single_Cell_Thunderstorm.png/750px-Typical_Lifecycle_of_a_Single_Cell_Thunderstorm.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Typical_Lifecycle_of_a_Single_Cell_Thunderstorm.png/1000px-Typical_Lifecycle_of_a_Single_Cell_Thunderstorm.png 2x" width="500"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Typical_Lifecycle_of_a_Single_Cell_Thunderstorm.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Progressive evolution of a Single Cell Thunderstorm</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
<div class="PopUpMediaTransform" id="mwe_player_2" style="width:300px;" videopayload='&lt;div class="mediaContainer" style="width:854px"&gt;&lt;video id="mwe_player_3" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Cloud_evolution_in_under_a_minute.ogv/854px--Cloud_evolution_in_under_a_minute.ogv.jpg" controls="" preload="none" autoplay="" style="width:854px;height:480px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="35.665850340136" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="Cloud_evolution_in_under_a_minute.ogv" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/2e/Cloud_evolution_in_under_a_minute.ogv" type="video/ogg; <div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cloud_evolution_in_under_a_minute.ogv" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Time-lapsed cloud evolution from cumulus humilis to cumulonimbus capillatus incus in under a minute.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Air can become saturated as a result of being cooled to its dew point or by having moisture added from an adjacent source. Adiabatic cooling occurs when one or more of three possible lifting agents – cyclonic/frontal, convective, or orographic – causes air containing invisible <a href="/wiki/Water_vapor" title="Water vapor">water vapor</a> to rise and cool to its <a href="/wiki/Dew_point" title="Dew point">dew point</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Temperature" title="Temperature">temperature</a> at which the air becomes saturated. The main mechanism behind this process is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adiabatic_lapse_rate#Dry_adiabatic_lapse_rate" title="Adiabatic lapse rate">adiabatic cooling</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-adiabatic_process_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-adiabatic_process-72">[72]</a></sup> If the air is cooled to its dew point and becomes saturated, it normally sheds vapor it can no longer retain, which condenses into cloud. Water vapor in saturated air is normally attracted to <a href="/wiki/Cloud_condensation_nuclei" title="Cloud condensation nuclei">condensation nuclei</a> such as dust and <a href="/wiki/Salt" title="Salt">salt</a> particles that are small enough to be held aloft by normal <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_circulation" title="Atmospheric circulation">circulation</a> of the air.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-humidity.2C_saturation.2C_and_stability_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-humidity.2C_saturation.2C_and_stability-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cloud_drops_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-cloud_drops-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<p>Frontal and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cyclonic" title="Cyclonic">cyclonic</a> lift occur when <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_instability" title="Atmospheric instability">stable</a> air is forced aloft at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Weather_fronts" title="Weather fronts">weather fronts</a> and around centers of <a href="/wiki/Low-pressure_area" title="Low-pressure area">low pressure</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-frontal_clouds_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-frontal_clouds-74">[74]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Warm_front" title="Warm front">Warm fronts</a> associated with extratropical cyclones tend to generate mostly cirriform and stratiform clouds over a wide area unless the approaching warm airmass is unstable, in which case cumulus congestus or cumulonimbus clouds will usually be embedded in the main precipitating cloud layer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mackerel_sky_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mackerel_sky-75">[75]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cold_front" title="Cold front">Cold fronts</a> are usually faster moving and generate a narrower line of clouds which are mostly stratocumuliform, cumuliform, or cumulonimbiform depending on the stability of the warm air mass just ahead of the front.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-G.26N:207-212_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-G.26N:207-212-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<p>Another agent is the convective upward motion of air caused by daytime solar heating at surface level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-humidity.2C_saturation.2C_and_stability_17-2"><a href="#cite_note-humidity.2C_saturation.2C_and_stability-17">[17]</a></sup> Airmass instability allows for the formation of cumuliform clouds that can produce showers if the air is sufficiently moist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> On comparatively rare occasions, convective lift can be powerful enough to penetrate the tropopause and push the cloud top into the stratosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tropopause_penetrations_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tropopause_penetrations-77">[77]</a></sup></p>
<p>A third source of lift is wind circulation forcing air over a physical barrier such as a <a href="/wiki/Mountain" title="Mountain">mountain</a> (<a href="/wiki/Orographic_lift" title="Orographic lift">orographic lift</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-humidity.2C_saturation.2C_and_stability_17-3"><a href="#cite_note-humidity.2C_saturation.2C_and_stability-17">[17]</a></sup> If the air is generally stable, nothing more than lenticular cap clouds will form. However, if the air becomes sufficiently moist and unstable, orographic showers or <a href="/wiki/Thunderstorm" title="Thunderstorm">thunderstorms</a> may appear.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MT_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-MT-78">[78]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dreamy_Twilight.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2112" data-file-width="2816" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Dreamy_Twilight.jpg/220px-Dreamy_Twilight.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Dreamy_Twilight.jpg/330px-Dreamy_Twilight.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Dreamy_Twilight.jpg/440px-Dreamy_Twilight.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dreamy_Twilight.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Windy evening <a href="/wiki/Twilight" title="Twilight">twilight</a> enhanced by the Sun's angle, can visually mimic a <a href="/wiki/Tornado" title="Tornado">tornado</a> resulting from orographic lift</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Along with adiabatic cooling that requires a lifting agent, there are three major non-adiabatic mechanisms for lowering the temperature of the air to its dew point. Conductive, radiational, and evaporative cooling require no lifting mechanism and can cause condensation at surface level resulting in the formation of <a href="/wiki/Fog" title="Fog">fog</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fog_formation_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-fog_formation-79">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup></p>
<p>There are several main sources of water vapor that can be added to the air as a way of achieving saturation without any cooling process: Water or moist ground,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-convection_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-convection-82">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> precipitation or virga,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Transpiration" title="Transpiration">transpiration</a> from plants<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Convergence_along_low-pressure_zones">Convergence along low-pressure zones</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cloud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Convergence along low-pressure zones">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intertropical_convergence_zone" title="Intertropical convergence zone">Intertropical convergence zone</a>, <a href="/wiki/Extratropical_cyclone" title="Extratropical cyclone">Extratropical cyclone</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cold_front" title="Cold front">Cold front</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Warm_front" title="Warm front">Warm front</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Worldclouds_2009.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="360" data-file-width="720" height="110" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Worldclouds_2009.jpg/220px-Worldclouds_2009.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Worldclouds_2009.jpg/330px-Worldclouds_2009.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Worldclouds_2009.jpg/440px-Worldclouds_2009.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>

</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
<div class="PopUpMediaTransform" id="mwe_player_4" style="width:220px;" videopayload='&lt;div class="mediaContainer" style="width:472px"&gt;&lt;video id="mwe_player_5" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/MODAL2_M_CLD_FR.ogv/472px--MODAL2_M_CLD_FR.ogv.jpg" controls="" preload="none" autoplay="" style="width:472px;height:316px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="26" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="MODAL2_M_CLD_FR.ogv" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/68/MODAL2_M_CLD_FR.ogv" type="video/ogg; codecs=&amp;quot;theora&amp;quot;" data-title="Original Ogg file, 472 × 316 (450 kbps)" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-width="472" data-height="316" data-bandwidth="449650" data-framerate="4"/&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/6/68/MODAL2_M_CLD_FR.ogv/MODAL2_M_CLD_FR.ogv.160p.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&amp;quot;vp8, vorbis&amp;quot;" data-title="Low bandwidth WebM (160P)" data-shorttitle="WebM 160P" data-transcodekey="160p.webm" data-width="238" data-height="160" data-bandwidth="128312" data-framerate="4"/&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/6/68/MODAL2_M_CLD_FR.ogv/MODAL2_M_CLD_FR.ogv.240p.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&amp;quot;vp8, vorbis&amp;quot;" data-title="Small WebM (240P)" data-shorttitle="WebM 240P" data-transcodekey="240p.webm" data-width="358" data-height="240" data-bandwidth="256208" data-framerate="4"/&gt;&lt;/video&gt;&lt;/div&gt;'><img alt="File:MODAL2 M CLD FR.ogv" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/MODAL2_M_CLD_FR.ogv/220px--MODAL2_M_CLD_FR.ogv.jpg" style="width:220px;height:147px"/><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/68/MODAL2_M_CLD_FR.ogv" target="new" title="Play media"><span class="play-btn-large"><span class="mw-tmh-playtext">Play media</span></span></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MODAL2_M_CLD_FR.ogv" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
These maps display the fraction of Earth's area that was cloudy on average during each month from January 2005 to August 2013. The measurements were collected by the Moderate Resolution Imaging Spectroradiometer (MODIS) on NASA's Terra satellite. Colors range from blue (no clouds) to white (totally cloudy). Like a digital camera, MODIS collects information in gridded boxes, or pixels. Cloud fraction is the portion of each pixel that is covered by clouds. Colors range from blue (no clouds) to white (totally cloudy).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> (<i>click for more detail</i>)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Although the local distribution of clouds can be significantly influenced by topography, the global prevalence of cloud cover tends to vary more by <a href="/wiki/Latitude" title="Latitude">latitude</a>. It is most prevalent globally in and along low pressure zones of surface atmospheric convergence which encircle the Earth close to the <a href="/wiki/Equator" title="Equator">equator</a> and near the 50th parallels of latitude in the northern and southern <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hemispheres_of_the_Earth" title="Hemispheres of the Earth">hemispheres</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> The adiabatic cooling processes that lead to the creation of clouds by way of lifting agents are all associated with convergence; a process that involves the horizontal inflow and accumulation of air at a given location, as well as the rate at which this happens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Convergence.2Fdivergence_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-Convergence.2Fdivergence-90">[90]</a></sup> Near the equator, increased cloudiness is due to the presence of the low-pressure <a href="/wiki/Intertropical_Convergence_Zone" title="Intertropical Convergence Zone">Intertropical Convergence Zone</a> (ITCZ) where very warm and unstable air promotes mostly cumuliform and cumulonimbiform clouds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-noaa_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-noaa-91">[91]</a></sup> Clouds of virtually any type can form along the mid-latitude convergence zones depending on the stability and moisture content of the air. These extratropical convergence zones are occupied by the <a href="/wiki/Polar_front" title="Polar front">polar fronts</a> where <a href="/wiki/Air_mass" title="Air mass">air masses</a> of polar origin meet and clash with those of tropical or subtropical origin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Climate_System:_General_Circulation_and_Climate_Zones_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Climate_System:_General_Circulation_and_Climate_Zones-92">[92]</a></sup> This leads to the formation of weather-making <a href="/wiki/Extratropical_cyclone" title="Extratropical cyclone">extratropical cyclones</a> composed of cloud systems that may be stable or unstable to varying degrees according to the stability characteristics of the various airmasses that are in conflict.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Divergence_along_high_pressure_zones">Divergence along high pressure zones</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cloud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Divergence along high pressure zones">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Subtropical_ridge" title="Subtropical ridge">Subtropical ridge</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polar_high" title="Polar high">Polar high</a></div>
<p>Divergence is the opposite of convergence. In the Earth's atmosphere, it involves the horizontal outflow of air from the upper part of a rising column of air, or from the lower part of a subsiding column often associated with an area or ridge of high pressure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Convergence.2Fdivergence_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-Convergence.2Fdivergence-90">[90]</a></sup> Cloudiness tends to be least prevalent near the poles and in the subtropics close to the 20th parallels, north and south. The latter are sometimes referred to as the <a href="/wiki/Horse_latitudes" title="Horse latitudes">horse latitudes</a>. The presence of a large-scale high-pressure <a href="/wiki/Subtropical_ridge" title="Subtropical ridge">subtropical ridge</a> on each side of the equator reduces cloudiness at these low latitudes. Similar patterns also occur at higher latitudes in both hemispheres.</p>
<h3><span id="Luminance,_reflectivity,_and_coloration"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Luminance.2C_reflectivity.2C_and_coloration">Luminance, reflectivity, and coloration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cloud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Luminance, reflectivity, and coloration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The luminance or brightness of a cloud is determined by how light is reflected, scattered, and transmitted by the cloud's particles. Its brightness may also be affected by the presence of haze or photometeors such as halos and rainbows.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Luminance_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-Luminance-94">[94]</a></sup> In the troposphere, dense, deep clouds exhibit a high reflectance (70% to 95%) throughout the <a href="/wiki/Visible_spectrum" title="Visible spectrum">visible spectrum</a>. Tiny particles of water are densely packed and sunlight cannot penetrate far into the cloud before it is reflected out, giving a cloud its characteristic white color, especially when viewed from the top.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Steven_Salter_and_John_Latham_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-Steven_Salter_and_John_Latham-95">[95]</a></sup> Cloud droplets tend to <a href="/wiki/Scattering" title="Scattering">scatter</a> light efficiently, so that the intensity of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_radiation" title="Solar radiation">solar radiation</a> decreases with depth into the gases. As a result, the <a href="/wiki/Cloud_base" title="Cloud base">cloud base</a> can vary from a very light to very-dark-grey depending on the cloud's thickness and how much <a href="/wiki/Light" title="Light">light</a> is being reflected or transmitted back to the observer. High thin tropospheric clouds reflect less light because of the comparatively low concentration of constituent ice crystals or supercooled water droplets which results in a slightly off-white appearance. However, a thick dense ice-crystal cloud appears brilliant white with pronounced grey shading because of its greater reflectivity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Luminance_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-Luminance-94">[94]</a></sup></p>
<p>As a tropospheric cloud matures, the dense water droplets may combine to produce larger droplets. If the droplets become too large and heavy to be kept aloft by the air circulation, they will fall from the cloud as <a href="/wiki/Rain" title="Rain">rain</a>. By this process of accumulation, the space between droplets becomes increasingly larger, permitting light to penetrate farther into the cloud. If the cloud is sufficiently large and the droplets within are spaced far enough apart, a percentage of the light that enters the cloud is not reflected back out but is absorbed giving the cloud a darker look. A simple example of this is one's being able to see farther in heavy rain than in heavy fog. This process of <a href="/wiki/Reflection_(physics)" title="Reflection (physics)">reflection</a>/<a href="/wiki/Absorption_(electromagnetic_radiation)" title="Absorption (electromagnetic radiation)">absorption</a> is what causes the range of cloud color from white to black.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bette_Hileman_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bette_Hileman-96">[96]</a></sup></p>
<p>Striking cloud colorations can be seen at any altitude, with the color of a cloud usually being the same as the incident light.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Coloration_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-Coloration-97">[97]</a></sup> During daytime when the sun is relatively high in the sky, tropospheric clouds generally appear bright white on top with varying shades of grey underneath. Thin clouds may look white or appear to have acquired the color of their <a href="/wiki/Natural_environment" title="Natural environment">environment</a> or background. Red, orange, and pink clouds occur almost entirely at sunrise/sunset and are the result of the scattering of sunlight by the atmosphere. When the sun is just below the horizon, low-level clouds are gray, middle clouds appear rose-colored, and high clouds are white or off-white. Clouds at night are black or dark grey in a moonless sky, or whitish when illuminated by the moon. They may also reflect the colors of large fires, city lights, or auroras that might be present.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Coloration_97-1"><a href="#cite_note-Coloration-97">[97]</a></sup></p>
<p>A cumulonimbus cloud that appears to have a greenish or bluish tint is a sign that it contains extremely high amounts of water; hail or rain which scatter light in a way that gives the cloud a blue color. A green colorization occurs mostly late in the day when the sun is comparatively low in the sky and the incident sunlight has a reddish tinge that appears green when illuminating a very tall bluish cloud. Supercell type storms are more likely to be characterized by this but any storm can appear this way. Coloration such as this does not directly indicate that it is a severe thunderstorm, it only confirms its potential. Since a green/blue tint signifies copious amounts of water, a strong updraft to support it, high winds from the storm raining out, and wet hail; all elements that improve the chance for it to become severe, can all be inferred from this. In addition, the stronger the updraft is, the more likely the storm is to undergo tornadogenesis and to produce large hail and high winds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Curiosities_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-Curiosities-98">[98]</a></sup></p>
<p>Yellowish clouds may be seen in the troposphere in the late spring through early fall months during <a href="/wiki/Wildfire" title="Wildfire">forest fire</a> season. The yellow color is due to the presence of pollutants in the smoke. Yellowish clouds are caused by the presence of nitrogen dioxide and are sometimes seen in urban areas with high air pollution levels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Garrett_Nagle_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-Garrett_Nagle-99">[99]</a></sup></p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Effects_on_climate_and_the_atmosphere">Effects on climate and the atmosphere</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cloud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Effects on climate and the atmosphere">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Cloud_cover" title="Cloud cover">Cloud cover</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cloud_feedback" title="Cloud feedback">Cloud feedback</a>, <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">Global warming</a>, <a href="/wiki/Global_dimming" title="Global dimming">Global dimming</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">Climate change</a></div>
<p>The role of tropospheric clouds in regulating <a href="/wiki/Weather" title="Weather">weather</a> and <a href="/wiki/Climate" title="Climate">climate</a> remains a leading source of uncertainty in <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a> projections.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> This uncertainty arises because of the delicate balance of processes related to clouds, spanning scales from millimeters to planetary. Hence, interactions between large-scale weather events (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Synoptic_meteorology" title="Synoptic meteorology">synoptic meteorology</a>) and clouds becomes difficult to represent in global models.</p>
<p>The complexity and diversity of clouds, as outlined above, adds to the problem. On the one hand, white-colored cloud tops promote cooling of Earth's surface by reflecting <i>short-wave</i> radiation from the sun. Most of the sunlight that reaches the ground is absorbed, warming the surface, which emits radiation upward at longer, <i>infrared</i>, wavelengths. At these wavelengths, however, water in the clouds acts as an efficient absorber. The water reacts by radiating, also in the infrared, both upward and downward, and the downward <i>long-wave</i> radiation results in some warming at the surface. This is analogous to the <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_effect" title="Greenhouse effect">greenhouse effect</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gases" title="Greenhouse gases">greenhouse gases</a> and <a href="/wiki/Water_vapor" title="Water vapor">water vapor</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cloud-heating_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-cloud-heating-102">[102]</a></sup></p>
<p>High-level genus-types particularly show this duality with both short-wave <a href="/wiki/Albedo" title="Albedo">albedo</a> cooling and long-wave greenhouse warming effects. On the whole though, <i>ice-crystal</i> clouds in the upper troposphere tend to favor net <i>warming</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clouds_and_the_greenhouse_effect_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-Clouds_and_the_greenhouse_effect-103">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nucleation_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nucleation-104">[104]</a></sup> However, the <i>cooling</i> effect is dominant with mid-level and low clouds made of very small <i>water droplets</i> with an average radius of about 2 µm (0.00008 in).,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cloud_drops_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-cloud_drops-73">[73]</a></sup> especially when they form in extensive sheets that block out more of the sun. Small-droplet aerosols are not good at absorbing long-wave radiation reflected back from Earth, so there is a net cooling with almost no long-wave effect. This effect is particularly pronounced with low clouds that form over water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clouds_and_the_greenhouse_effect_103-1"><a href="#cite_note-Clouds_and_the_greenhouse_effect-103">[103]</a></sup> Measurements taken by NASA indicate that on the whole, the effects of low and mid-level clouds that tend to promote cooling are outweighing the warming effects of high layers and the variable outcomes associated with or vertically developed clouds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clouds_and_the_greenhouse_effect_103-2"><a href="#cite_note-Clouds_and_the_greenhouse_effect-103">[103]</a></sup></p>
<p>Low and vertical heaps of <i>cumulus</i>, <i>towering cumulus</i>, and <i>cumulonimbus</i> are made of larger water droplets ranging in radius from 5 to about 15 µm. Nimbostratus cloud droplets can also be quite large, up to 15 µm radius.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-large_droplets_in_precipitating_clouds_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-large_droplets_in_precipitating_clouds-105">[105]</a></sup> These larger droplets associated with vertically developed clouds are better able to trap the long-wave radiation thus mitigating the cooling effect to some degree. However, these large often precipitating clouds are variable or unpredictable in their overall effect because of variations in their concentration, distribution, and vertical extent.</p>
<p>As difficult as it is to evaluate the effects of current cloud cover characteristics on climate change, it is even more problematic to predict the outcome of this change with respect to future cloud patterns and events. As a consequence, much research has focused on the response of low and vertical clouds to a changing climate. Leading global models can produce quite different results, however, with some showing increasing low-étage clouds and others showing decreases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup></p>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For Global brightening, see <a href="/wiki/Global_dimming" title="Global dimming">Global dimming</a>.</div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Above_the_troposphere">Above the troposphere</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cloud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Above the troposphere">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Polar_stratospheric">Polar stratospheric</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cloud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Polar stratospheric">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nacreous_clouds_Antarctica.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Nacreous_clouds_Antarctica.jpg/220px-Nacreous_clouds_Antarctica.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Nacreous_clouds_Antarctica.jpg/330px-Nacreous_clouds_Antarctica.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Nacreous_clouds_Antarctica.jpg/440px-Nacreous_clouds_Antarctica.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nacreous_clouds_Antarctica.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Stratospheric nacreous clouds over Antarctica</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Polar_stratospheric_cloud" title="Polar stratospheric cloud">Polar stratospheric clouds</a> show little variation in structure and are limited to a single very high range of altitude of about 15,000–25,000 m (49,200–82,000 ft), so they are not classified into altitude levels, genus types, species, or varieties in the manner of tropospheric clouds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Polar-stratospheric_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-Polar-stratospheric-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>Polar stratospheric clouds form in the lowest part of the stratosphere during the <a href="/wiki/Winter" title="Winter">winter</a>, at the altitude and during the season that produces the coldest temperatures and therefore the best chances of triggering condensation caused by adiabatic cooling. They are typically very thin with an undulating cirriform appearance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-108">[108]</a></sup> Moisture is scarce in the stratosphere, so nacreous and non-nacreous cloud at this altitude range is rare and is usually restricted to polar regions in the winter where the air is coldest.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Polar_mesospheric">Polar mesospheric</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cloud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Polar mesospheric">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Helkivad_%C3%B6%C3%B6pilved_Kuresoo_kohal.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2261" data-file-width="3402" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Helkivad_%C3%B6%C3%B6pilved_Kuresoo_kohal.jpg/220px-Helkivad_%C3%B6%C3%B6pilved_Kuresoo_kohal.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Helkivad_%C3%B6%C3%B6pilved_Kuresoo_kohal.jpg/330px-Helkivad_%C3%B6%C3%B6pilved_Kuresoo_kohal.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Helkivad_%C3%B6%C3%B6pilved_Kuresoo_kohal.jpg/440px-Helkivad_%C3%B6%C3%B6pilved_Kuresoo_kohal.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Helkivad_%C3%B6%C3%B6pilved_Kuresoo_kohal.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Noctilucent_cloud" title="Noctilucent cloud">Noctilucent cloud</a> over Estonia</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polar_mesospheric_cloud" title="Polar mesospheric cloud">Polar mesospheric clouds</a> form at a single extreme altitude range of about 80 to 85 km (50 to 53 mi) and are consequently not classified into more than one level. They are given the Latin name <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Noctilucent_clouds" title="Noctilucent clouds">noctilucent</a> because of their illumination well after sunset and before sunrise. They typically have a bluish or silvery white coloration that can resemble brightly illuminated cirrus. Noctilucent clouds may occasionally take on more of a red or orange hue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Noctilucent_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-Noctilucent-109">[109]</a></sup> They are not common or widespread enough to have a significant effect on climate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-simulation_studies_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-simulation_studies-110">[110]</a></sup> However, an increasing frequency of occurrence of noctilucent clouds since the 19th century may be the result of climate change.</p>
<p>Noctilucent clouds are the highest in the atmosphere and form near the top of the mesosphere at about ten times the altitude of tropospheric high clouds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-111">[111]</a></sup> From ground level, they can occasionally be seen illuminated by the sun during deep <a href="/wiki/Twilight" title="Twilight">twilight</a>. Ongoing research indicates that convective lift in the mesosphere is strong enough during the polar <a href="/wiki/Summer" title="Summer">summer</a> to cause adiabatic cooling of small amount of water vapour to the point of saturation. This tends to produce the coldest temperatures in the entire atmosphere just below the mesopause. These conditions result in the best environment for the formation of polar mesospheric clouds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-simulation_studies_110-1"><a href="#cite_note-simulation_studies-110">[110]</a></sup> There is also evidence that smoke particles from burnt-up meteors provide much of the condensation nuclei required for the formation of noctilucent cloud.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sounding_rocket_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-sounding_rocket-112">[112]</a></sup></p>
<p>Distribution in the mesosphere is similar to the stratosphere except at much higher altitudes. Because of the need for maximum cooling of the water vapor to produce noctilucent clouds, their distribution tends to be restricted to polar regions of Earth. A major seasonal difference is that convective lift from below the mesosphere pushes very scarce water vapor to higher colder altitudes required for cloud formation during the respective summer seasons in the northern and southern hemispheres. Sightings are rare more than 45 degrees south of the north pole or north of the south pole.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Noctilucent_109-1"><a href="#cite_note-Noctilucent-109">[109]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Extraterrestrial">Extraterrestrial</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cloud&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Extraterrestrial">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extraterrestrial_atmospheres" title="Extraterrestrial atmospheres">Extraterrestrial atmospheres</a></div>
<p>Cloud cover has been seen on most other planets in the solar system. <a href="/wiki/Venus" title="Venus">Venus</a>'s thick clouds are composed of <a href="/wiki/Sulfur_dioxide" title="Sulfur dioxide">sulfur dioxide</a> (due to volcanic activity) and appear to be almost entirely stratiform.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bougher-127-129_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bougher-127-129-113">[113]</a></sup> They are arranged in three main layers at altitudes of 45 to 65 km that obscure the <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">planet</a>'s surface and can produce <a href="/wiki/Virga" title="Virga">virga</a>. No embedded cumuliform types have been identified, but broken stratocumuliform wave formations are sometimes seen in the top layer that reveal more continuous layer clouds underneath.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mysterious_waves_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mysterious_waves-114">[114]</a></sup> On <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a>, noctilucent, cirrus, cirrocumulus and stratocumulus composed of water-ice have been detected mostly near the poles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-photo_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-photo-116">[116]</a></sup> Water-ice fogs have also been detected on Mars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mars-clouds_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mars-clouds-117">[117]</a></sup></p>
<p>Both <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a> and <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">Saturn</a> have an outer cirriform cloud deck composed of ammonia,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> an intermediate stratiform haze-cloud layer made of <a href="/wiki/Ammonium_hydrosulfide" title="Ammonium hydrosulfide">ammonium hydrosulfide</a>, and an inner deck of cumulus water clouds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> Embedded cumulonimbus are known to exist near the <a href="/wiki/Great_Red_Spot" title="Great Red Spot">Great Red Spot</a> on <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jupiter-clouds_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jupiter-clouds-122">[122]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Saturn-clouds_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-Saturn-clouds-123">[123]</a></sup> The same category-types can be found covering <a href="/wiki/Uranus" title="Uranus">Uranus</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">Neptune</a>, but are all composed of <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Neptune.27s_Atmosphere_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-Neptune.27s_Atmosphere-124">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Uranus-clouds_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-Uranus-clouds-125">[125]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Irwin-115_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-Irwin-115-126">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lunine_1993_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lunine_1993-128">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-elkins-tanton_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-elkins-tanton-129">[129]</a></sup> Saturn's moon <a href="/wiki/Titan_(moon)" title="Titan (moon)">Titan</a> has cirrus clouds believed to be composed largely of methane.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Coustenis155_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-Coustenis155-130">[130]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Cassini%E2%80%93Huygens" title="Cassini–Huygens">Cassini–Huygens</a> Saturn mission uncovered evidence of polar stratospheric clouds<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clouds_on_titan_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-Clouds_on_titan-132">[132]</a></sup> and a fluid cycle on Titan, including lakes near the poles and fluvial channels on the surface of the moon.</p>
<p>Some planets outside the solar system are known to have atmospheric clouds. In October 2013, the detection of high altitude optically thick clouds in the <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">atmosphere</a> of <a href="/wiki/Exoplanet" title="Exoplanet">exoplanet</a> <a href="/wiki/Kepler-7b" title="Kepler-7b">Kepler-7b</a> was announced,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MIT-20131002_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-MIT-20131002-133">[133]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> and, in December 2013, in the atmospheres of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GJ_436_b" title="GJ 436 b">GJ 436 b</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GJ_1214_b" title="GJ 1214 b">GJ 1214 b</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20131231_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20131231-135">[135]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NAT-20140101a_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-NAT-20140101a-136">[136]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NAT-20140101b_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-NAT-20140101b-137">[137]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NAT-20140101c_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-NAT-20140101c-138">[138]</a></sup></p>

<li style="display:table-row"><span style="display:table-cell;padding:0.2em;vertical-align:middle;text-align:center"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cumulus_clouds_in_fair_weather.jpeg"><img alt="icon" class="noviewer" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="800" height="24" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Cumulus_clouds_in_fair_weather.jpeg/32px-Cumulus_clouds_in_fair_weather.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Cumulus_clouds_in_fair_weather.jpeg/48px-Cumulus_clouds_in_fair_weather.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Cumulus_clouds_in_fair_weather.jpeg/64px-Cumulus_clouds_in_fair_weather.jpeg 2x" width="32"/></a></span><span style="display:table-cell;padding:0.2em 0.2em 0.2em 0.3em;vertical-align:middle"><a href="/wiki/Portal:Weather" title="Portal:Weather">Weather portal</a></span></li>
